## Iteration N - Story: US-078 Site-Wide: Micro-Interactions and Scroll-Triggered Polish

### What was implemented
- `menu-card.tsx`: Removed JS-based `addScaling` state; replaced with `active:scale-95` CSS pseudo-class on all three add/increment buttons; fixed decrement button from `active:scale-90` → `active:scale-95`
- `globals.css`: Added global button `active:scale-95` fallback in `@layer utilities` for buttons without explicit transform utilities
- Previous iterations had already implemented: `imgLoaded` skeleton (animate-pulse on container until image loads + `onLoad` callback), `badgeBounce` wired to tray badge span, filter chips with `transition-all duration-200 active:scale-95 scale-[1.04]` on active state, global prefers-reduced-motion override

### Patterns discovered
- Most of US-078 was pre-implemented in previous passes: skeleton, badge bounce, filter chip animations
- The `addScaling` JS pattern (scale-110 on click via useState) conflicts with CSS `active:scale-95` because `scale-100` utility class is always present and overrides base layer rules; simplest fix is to remove JS animation and use pure CSS `active:scale-95`
- menu-filter.tsx already had `scale-[1.04]` on selected chip state + `transition-all duration-200` — effectively a "scale up on active" animation

### Gotchas
- `@layer base` rules can be overridden by utility classes (scale-100 etc.) — use `@layer utilities` for the global button rule instead, or add `active:scale-95` directly to button className
- The global `button:not(:disabled):active` rule goes in `@layer utilities` to ensure it applies broadly

    ```javascript
    useEffect(() => {
      // Call the test fetching logic here
    }, []);
    ```

4.  Implement `useState` variables to track `isLoading` and `error` states.

6.  Remove the now-redundant "Load Tests" button from the JSX.

*   **Error Handling:** If the fetch fails, set an error state and display a "Retry" button as per acceptance criteria. The retry button should re-trigger the fetch.
*   **State Management:** Ensure the component correctly transitions between loading, error, and success states without race conditions.
*   **Button Confusion:** The story distinguishes between the old "Load Tests" button and the main "Run All Tests" button. Be careful to only remove the former.

1.  **Files to Modify/Create:**
    *   **Create:** `src/app/admin/[[...tab]]/page.tsx`. This single dynamic route will handle all admin tabs.
    *   **Modify:** The component responsible for rendering the admin sidebar/navigation (likely in `src/components/admin/`). Update its navigation items from `onClick` handlers to Next.js `<Link>` components (e.g., `<Link href="/admin/menu">`).

    *   **Delete/Redirect:** The existing `src/app/admin/page.tsx` will be superseded by the new dynamic route.

2.  **Key Framework Features & Hooks:**
    *   **Next.js App Router:** Use a dynamic catch-all route (`[[...tab]]`).
    *   **React Hooks:** Use the `useParams` hook in the new page to get the `tab` slug from the URL.
    *   **Component Props:** Pass the slug down as an `initialTab` prop.
    *   **Client-side Navigation:** Use the `next/link` component for seamless URL updates without page reloads.

3.  **Recommended Approach:**
    *   Implement the `src/app/admin/[[...tab]]/page.tsx` dynamic route.
    *   This page component will extract the `tab` parameter from its `params` prop.
    *   It should map the URL slug (e.g., "menu") to the corresponding tab's title or key (e.g., "Menu").
    *   Render the main admin page/layout component, passing the resolved tab name as the `initialTab` prop.
    *   Ensure the authentication logic currently protecting `/admin` is applied to this new dynamic route.

    *   **Root URL:** Handle requests to `/admin` (where the `tab` param will be undefined). Default to a primary tab like 'menu'.
    *   **Invalid URL:** If a user navigates to an invalid sub-URL (e.g., `/admin/nonexistent`), gracefully default to the primary tab instead of crashing.
    *   **Auth:** Verify that the authentication check (e.g., token validation, redirect) is correctly inherited or moved to the new `[[...tab]]` route to secure all sub-pages.

*   **Routing:** Next.js App Router with dynamic segments (`[[...tab]]`). The `params` object in the page component will be used to get the active tab from the URL.
*   **Components:** The main `AdminPage` will be rendered by the new dynamic route, passing the tab from the URL as a prop.
*   **State Management:** The `AdminTabs` component will use the `initialTab` prop to initialize its internal `useState` for the active tab.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  Create the `src/app/admin/[[...tab]]/page.tsx` file.
2.  Inside this server component, read the `params.tab` array. The first element (`tab?.[0]`) is the slug (e.g., "menu").
3.  Map the slug to the correct tab name (e.g., 'menu' -> 'Menu'). Handle the default case where `tab` is undefined, setting a default like 'Menu'.
4.  Copy the authentication logic from the old `src/app/admin/page.tsx` to the new dynamic route to ensure all sub-pages are auth-gated.
5.  Render the main `AdminPage` or `AdminTabs` component, passing the resolved tab name as the `initialTab` prop.
6.  Update the sidebar navigation to use `<Link href="/admin/...">` instead of state-setting `onClick` handlers to ensure URL changes and proper navigation.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Invalid Slugs:** If a user navigates to `/admin/non-existent-page`, the logic in `[[...tab]]/page.tsx` must handle it gracefully, likely by defaulting to the primary tab (e.g., 'Menu').
*   **URL/State Desync:** Ensure that the component holding tab state correctly initializes from the `initialTab` prop and does not rely on its own default `useState` value when a URL parameter is present.
*   **Base Route:** The logic must correctly handle the base `/admin` route (where `params.tab` will be `undefined`) and select a default tab.

- `params` in Next.js App Router is a `Promise` in newer versions — must `await params` to destructure

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**

*   **Special Characters:** The "Featured" column is represented by a star (★). Ensure the key used in the `COLUMN_TOOLTIPS` object matches how this column is identified in the code (e.g., 'STAR' or 'FEATURED').
*   **Accessibility:** Using the `shadcn/ui` Tooltip component is preferred as it handles accessibility (ARIA attributes) correctly. If using a simple `title` attribute for a quicker implementation, ensure it's applied to an element that can receive focus.

Here is the technical brief for implementing the KDS user story in a Frappe/Python environment.

---

**TO:** Claude Code Agent
**RE:** Technical Brief for US-087: KDS Implementation in Frappe

This brief outlines the Python/Frappe implementation for the Kitchen Display System (KDS) user story. The goal is a read-only, auto-refreshing web view at the `/kds` endpoint for kitchen staff.

**1. Python Files & Structure:**
-   **`[app_name]/www/kds/kds.py`**: To handle server-side logic. It will contain the whitelisted function to fetch orders and the `get_context` method to handle authentication checks.
-   **`[app_name]/www/kds/kds.html`**: A Jinja2 template for the UI. It will conditionally render either a login form or the order queue based on the user's session.
-   **`[app_name]/www/kds/kds.js`**: Frontend JavaScript to handle the 30-second auto-refresh via `frappe.call` and dynamically render the fetched order data.
-   **`[app_name]/hooks.py`**: Use the `after_install` hook to create the "Kitchen Staff" role and seed the initial 'kitchen' user.

**2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:**
-   **DocTypes**:
    -   `Sales Order` (or equivalent): The primary DocType to query. It must have a `status` field (e.g., 'Preparing') and fields for ETA and order items.
    -   `User` & `Role`: A new `Role` named "Kitchen Staff" should be created to provide scoped, read-only access.
-   **Whitelisted API**: The function in `kds.py` fetching orders must be decorated with `@frappe.whitelist()`.

**3. Implementation Approach:**
1.  Define the "Kitchen Staff" `Role` and grant it read-only access to the `Sales Order` DocType, filtered where `status` is 'Preparing'.
2.  In `kds.py`, the `get_context` function will check if `frappe.session.user` has the "Kitchen Staff" role. If not, it renders the login portion of the template.
3.  The whitelisted function will use `frappe.get_all()` to query for orders, returning key details as JSON.
4.  The `kds.js` file will use `setInterval` to call the whitelisted function and update the DOM.

**4. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
-   **Authentication**: Do not implement custom auth. Use Frappe's built-in `User` model. The login form should submit to the standard Frappe login handler.
-   **Security**: The kitchen user's role must be strictly read-only to prevent unintended modifications.
-   **Credentials**: The default kitchen user and password should be seeded during installation from `frappe.conf` or a custom Site Setting, not hardcoded in the Python source.
-   **Performance**: Ensure the `status` field on the `Sales Order` DocType is indexed to handle frequent polling efficiently.


## Pre-analysis for US-087 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This is a technical brief for implementing the KDS feature described in story US-087. While the request mentions Frappe/Python, the story's technical notes and project structure clearly indicate a Next.js/TypeScript implementation.

***

**Technical Brief: US-087 - Kitchen Display System (Next.js)**

**1. Core Files to Create:**
*   `src/app/kds/page.tsx`: Main route. Will conditionally render the login form or the order queue based on authentication status.
*   `src/app/api/kds/login/route.ts`: Handles POST requests for KDS authentication. Validates credentials against environment variables and returns a JWT.
*   `src/app/api/kds/orders/route.ts`: A protected GET endpoint that fetches and returns orders where `status` is `'preparing'`.
*   A client component for the order queue, e.g., `src/components/kds/OrderQueue.tsx`.
*   `.env.example`: Update with `KITCHEN_USERNAME` and `KITCHEN_PASSWORD`.

**2. Involved Logic & Data Structures:**
*   **Data Model:** Requires querying the primary `orders` data source.
*   **API Routes:** The implementation centers around the two new API routes for KDS-specific authentication and data fetching.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   **Authentication:** Use a dedicated JWT, separate from admin sessions. The `/api/kds/login` route should issue this token and set it in a secure, `HttpOnly` cookie.
*   **Routing/Protection:** Use `middleware.ts` to protect the `/api/kds/orders` route, requiring the KDS-specific auth cookie. The `/kds` page itself can read the cookie to determine which component to show.
*   **UI (Client-Side):** The `OrderQueue` component should use a `useEffect` hook with `setInterval` to poll the `/api/kds/orders` endpoint every 30 seconds.
*   **Styling:** Employ a high-contrast, large-font design using Tailwind CSS, optimized for tablet viewing in a kitchen environment.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Auth State:** The KDS authentication token/cookie must not grant access to any other admin part of the application.
*   **Resource Cleanup:** The `setInterval` in the `useEffect` hook must be cleared on component unmount to prevent memory leaks.
*   **Error Handling:** Implement clear feedback for failed login attempts and for errors during the auto-refresh data fetch.
*   **Data Latency:** While polling is specified, for a real-world scenario, consider Server-Sent Events (SSE) for more efficient, real-time order updates.

## Pre-analysis for US-088 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story appears to be for a **Next.js/React frontend**, not a Frappe/Python backend. The technical notes reference React components (`onClick`, `className`), JSX syntax, client-side state (`Map<orderId, Set<itemIndex>>`), and a REST API endpoint (`PATCH /api/...`), all of which are characteristic of a modern web frontend framework.

Here is a technical brief based on the Next.js/React architecture suggested by the story and your project files.

***

**Technical Brief: US-088 KDS Item Completion**

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   **Frontend Page:** `src/app/kds/page.tsx` (or similar) - To manage the state of completed items across all orders.
*   **Frontend Component:** `src/components/kds/OrderCard.tsx` - The component that renders an individual order card. It will need logic to check if all its items are complete.
*   **Frontend Component:** A new or existing `src/components/kds/OrderItem.tsx` - To handle the individual item's display, tap interaction, and completed status visuals (strikethrough/check).
*   **Backend API Route:** `src/app/api/admin/orders/[id]/route.ts` - The server-side handler for the `PATCH` request to update the order status.

**2. Key DocTypes & Hooks (React/Next.js equivalent):**
*   **Frontend State Hook:** Use `useState` in the main KDS page component to hold a `Map` tracking completed items (e.g., `useState(new Map<string, Set<number>>())`).
*   **API Interaction:** A client-side `fetch` call to the `PATCH` API route.
*   **Database Model:** The API route will interact with the `Order` model in your database (likely via Prisma or a similar ORM) to change its `status` field to 'ready'.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  In `kds/page.tsx`, define a state `const [completedItems, setCompletedItems] = useState(new Map())`.
2.  Create a `toggleItem(orderId, itemIndex)` function that updates this map. Pass this function down to `OrderCard`.
3.  In `OrderCard`, determine if all items for its order are complete by checking against the `completedItems` prop.
4.  Conditionally render a "Mark as Ready" button if all items are complete. This button will trigger a `markAsReady(orderId)` function.
5.  The `markAsReady` function will make the `PATCH` request. On success, it should optimistically remove the order from the local state to update the UI instantly.
6.  The `OrderItem` component will use the `toggleItem` handler and apply conditional styling (`line-through`, icons) based on its completed state.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **State Persistence:** The spec says state is local. If the user refreshes the page, the completion status of individual items will be lost. This is acceptable per the requirements but is a key UX point.
*   **API Error Handling:** If the `PATCH` request to "Mark as Ready" fails, the UI must handle it gracefully (e.g., show a toast notification) and not remove the order card.
*   **Race Conditions:** Not a major concern since item state is local, but the final `PATCH` should ensure the order is still in a 'preparing' state before updating it to 'ready'.

**Subject: Technical Brief for US-089: KDS Real-Time Order Updates**

This brief outlines the backend implementation for real-time Kitchen Display System (KDS) order updates within the Frappe framework.

**1. Python Files to Modify:**
*   `[kds_app]/hooks.py`: To identify the whitelisted API method that fetches KDS orders.
*   `[kds_app]/api.py` or `[kds_app]/utils/orders.py`: The file containing the implementation of the whitelisted function (e.g., `get_preparing_orders`). This is the primary file to edit.

**2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:**
*   **DocType:** A custom DocType, likely named `Kitchen Order` or `Sales Order`, which must contain a `status` field (e.g., 'To Do', 'Preparing', 'Ready').
*   **Hook:** A `@frappe.whitelist()` decorated Python function that is called via a `frappe.call` from the frontend to fetch the list of active orders.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   The existing whitelisted function should be optimized to query the Order DocType.
*   The query must filter for documents where `status` is 'Preparing' (or the equivalent active state).
*   The function should return a lean JSON response containing only the fields essential for the KDS display (order ID, items, quantities, notes) to ensure fast responses for the 15-second polling interval. The frontend will be responsible for comparing the new list with the old one to identify new orders.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Performance:** With polling every 15 seconds, the database query must be highly efficient. Ensure the `status` field on the Order DocType is indexed (`"indexed": 1` in the DocType's `.json` file).
*   **Data Payload:** Avoid returning the full Frappe document (`frappe.get_doc`). Instead, use `frappe.get_all` or a direct `frappe.db.sql` query to select only the necessary fields, minimizing payload size.
*   **Status Names:** Verify the exact string values for the statuses ('Preparing', 'Ready', etc.) from the DocType definition to ensure the filter is correct.

This technical brief is for a **Next.js/TypeScript** project, not a Frappe/Python one as mentioned in the prompt. The following plan is based on the details in the provided JSON user story.

**Project:** Next.js AI Waiter
**Objective:** Replace static menu file with a live, cached database query for the AI's system prompt.

*   `src/lib/chat/system-prompt.ts`: Core logic for building the prompt. This is where the new DB fetching, caching, and formatting logic will reside.
*   `src/app/api/chat/route.ts`: Main chat endpoint. Needs modification to `await` the now-asynchronous `getSystemPrompt()` function.
*   `src/app/api/admin/menu/[id]/route.ts`: Menu update endpoint. Will be modified to call the cache invalidation function after a successful item update.

**2. Involved Components (Next.js context):**
*   **Database Table:** `menu_items` in the Neon DB is the source of truth.
*   **API Routes:** `POST /api/chat` (consumes the prompt) and `PATCH /api/admin/menu/[id]` (triggers cache invalidation).

**3. Implementation Approach:**

## Pre-analysis for US-092 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This is a technical brief for a TypeScript/Next.js project, based on the `technicalNotes` in the JSON provided.

**Technical Brief: US-092 - AI Waiter Availability Logic**

**1. Primary Files to Modify:**
*   `src/lib/chat/system-prompt.ts`: This file is the main target. It will need new logic to process and partition the menu items before injecting them into the prompt.

*   **Menu Item Type:** The TypeScript type for menu items, which includes `available: boolean`, pricing, and filtering criteria (e.g., available times/days).
*   **System Prompt:** The string template that is dynamically assembled and sent to the AI model.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  **Data Fetching:** Alter the DB query function to return all menu items, regardless of their `available` status or time-based rules.
2.  **Item Partitioning:** In `system-prompt.ts`, before building the prompt, filter the full item list into two distinct arrays:
    *   `availableNow`: Items where `available: true` and all time/day rules currently pass.
    *   `unavailableToday`: Items where `available: false` OR which fail the current time/day rules.
3.  **Prompt Injection:** Structure the prompt to include two clearly demarcated lists:
    *   A section titled `CURRENTLY AVAILABLE ITEMS:` containing the `availableNow` list.
    *   A section titled `ITEMS NOT AVAILABLE TODAY:` containing the `unavailableToday` list.
4.  **Instruction Update:** Modify the prompt's core instructions with a rule like: "If a user asks about an item in the `unavailableToday` list, you must say it is not available today and suggest an alternative from the `availableNow` list. Never say the cafe does not have an item if it appears in either list."

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   The filtering logic must correctly distinguish between items that are permanently disabled (`available: false`) and those temporarily unavailable due to time rules. Both belong in the "unavailable" list for the AI's context.
*   The prompt instructions must be explicit and unambiguous to ensure the AI correctly follows the new "unavailable today" vs. "not on menu" logic.
*   This implementation is critically dependent on the upstream change to the database query from US-090.

*   **`next/dynamic`:** Use to defer loading of non-critical, below-the-fold components (e.g., footer, modals).

*   **Total Blocking Time (TBT):** The `ChatWidget` is a known issue that hurts TBT. Per technical notes, deferring this is part of US-095, so focus on other TBT wins.
*   **Cumulative Layout Shift (CLS):** Be vigilant. CLS can be triggered by web fonts, ads, or images without dimensions. Check that aspect ratios are maintained during load.

### **US-103: Technical Implementation Brief**

**Objective:** Implement a client-side, one-time splash screen for first-time visitors.

**2. Core Logic & Hooks:**
*   The splash component will manage its own visibility using `useState` and `useEffect`.
*   On mount (`useEffect`), it must check `localStorage` for a `mm_splash_seen` flag.
*   If the flag is present, the component should render `null` or immediately hide itself.
*   The "Dismiss" button's `onClick` handler will set `localStorage.setItem('mm_splash_seen', '1')` and update the component's state to trigger a CSS-based exit animation.

**3. Recommended Implementation:**
*   In `splash-onboarding.tsx`, create a state `const [isVisible, setIsVisible] = useState(false)`.
*   The `useEffect` hook will check `localStorage` and set `setIsVisible(true)` only if the flag is *not* found.
*   Use Tailwind CSS for a `fixed inset-0 z-50` overlay.
*   Use Lucide React (`lucide-react`) for icons like `Utensils`, `Send`, and `Smile`.
*   Render `<SplashOnboarding />` unconditionally in `page.tsx`; the component's internal logic will control its appearance.

**4. Edge Cases to Watch For:**
*   **SSR Flash:** The component must default to a hidden state to avoid a flash of content during Server-Side Rendering, as `localStorage` is only available on the client. The `useEffect` hook correctly handles this.
*   **No Translation:** Per notes, hardcode English text; no i18n is needed for this component.

## Pre-analysis for US-103 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
### Technical Brief: US-103 First-Time Visitor Splash Page

**Project Technology:** Next.js / React / TypeScript (Not Frappe/Python)

**1. Files to Create / Modify:**
*   **Create:** `src/components/home/splash-onboarding.tsx`. This new client component (`'use client'`) will contain all logic and UI for the splash screen.
*   **Modify:** `src/app/[locale]/page.tsx` (the homepage). Import and render the `<SplashOnboarding />` component here.

**2. Key APIs and Hooks:**
*   **React Hooks:** `useState` to manage the splash's visibility state; `useEffect` to safely access browser APIs on component mount.
*   **Browser API:** `window.localStorage` to check for and set the `mm_splash_seen` flag, ensuring persistence.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  In `splash-onboarding.tsx`, use a `useEffect` hook with an empty dependency array (`[]`) to run a check on mount.
2.  Inside the effect, read `localStorage.getItem('mm_splash_seen')`.
3.  If the key is `null`, use a state setter (e.g., `setShowSplash(true)`) to trigger the rendering of the splash modal.
4.  The dismiss button's `onClick` handler will set `localStorage.setItem('mm_splash_seen', '1')` and then set state to hide the modal.
5.  Render the component unconditionally in the homepage; its internal logic will control its visibility.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **SSR Compatibility:** Accessing `localStorage` must be wrapped in `useEffect` to prevent "localStorage is not defined" errors during server-side rendering.
*   **No Flash of Content:** The component should render `null` by default until the `useEffect` check confirms the splash needs to be shown, preventing unwanted layout shifts.
*   **Styling:** Use a high `z-index` (e.g., `z-50`) with a `fixed inset-0` class to ensure the splash covers the entire viewport.

## Pre-analysis for US-104 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
Here is the technical brief for user story US-104.

**Objective:** Implement equal-width menu cards in the category grid on the `/en/menu` page, ensuring long item names wrap instead of expanding the card.

**1. Primary Files to Modify:**
*   `src/components/menu/menu-grid.tsx`: This is the main component rendering the menu item grid.
*   `src/components/menu/menu-card.tsx`: The individual card component will likely need style adjustments for text wrapping.
*   `src/app/[locale]/menu/page.tsx`: Verify how `menu-grid.tsx` is used and if any parent styles interfere.

**2. Key Components & Frameworks:**
*   **React Component:** `MenuGrid` and `MenuCard` (or similarly named components).
*   **Styling:** Tailwind CSS.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  In `menu-grid.tsx`, modify the `div` that acts as the grid container.
2.  Set explicit, responsive column counts. Remove any `minmax` or `auto-fit` properties.
    *   **Example:** `grid grid-cols-2 md:grid-cols-3 lg:grid-cols-4 gap-4`
3.  In `menu-card.tsx`, ensure the root element of the card has `w-full` to make it fill its assigned grid column.
4.  Locate the text element for the item name. Apply `break-words` to handle wrapping.
5.  For a cleaner look, consider using the `@tailwindcss/line-clamp` plugin to truncate text to a specific number of lines (e.g., `line-clamp-2`). This also helps maintain a more consistent card height.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Variable Card Height:** Forcing equal width can lead to unequal heights if item names wrap to 2 vs. 3 lines. To fix this, apply a `min-height` to the text container or the entire card to normalize row heights.
*   **Line Clamp Plugin:** If the `@tailwindcss/line-clamp` plugin is not installed, it will need to be added via `npm` or `yarn` and configured in `tailwind.config.js`.
*   **Visual Regression:** Test across all specified breakpoints (390px, 768px, 1024px) to confirm the column layout is correct and no horizontal scrollbar appears.
*   **Image Distortion:** Ensure images within the cards continue to maintain their correct aspect ratio (e.g., using `aspect-square` and `object-cover`).

- `menu-card.tsx`: added `w-full overflow-hidden` to card class string to enforce equal-width fill

- Grid breakpoints: mobile `grid-cols-2`, `md:` (768px) for 3-col, `lg:` (1024px) for 4-col — matches acceptance criteria
- `sm:` (640px) breakpoint is skipped; going from 2-col directly to 3-col at 768px

### Gotchas
- Flat grid (search/display category results) had NO base column count — was 1-col on mobile despite appearing to be a 2-col layout at sm
- Both grids needed the same column progression to be consistent

## Iteration — Story: US-093 Lighthouse Mobile Score >= 85 (LCP, CLS, FCP, TBT)

### What was implemented
1. **hero-section.tsx**: Removed `animate-fade-in` class from the mobile hero image container div. The LCP image (hero-mobile.webp) was starting at `opacity: 0` due to the CSS animation (`animation: fade-in ... both`), causing Chrome to defer LCP measurement until the animation completed (~500ms). Now the container is immediately visible, allowing LCP to register on first paint.
2. **globals.css**: Moved `body { transition: background-color..., color... }` into `@media (prefers-reduced-motion: no-preference)` block. This is the correct pattern — theme transitions only apply when motion is allowed, matching the existing global `animation-duration: 0.01ms !important` override.

### Lighthouse Results
- **No throttle (warm cache, production server):** Performance 96, LCP 1.9s ✓, CLS 0.016 ✓, FCP 0.8s ✓, TBT 230ms ✓
- **Simulated mobile throttle (localhost `next start`):** Performance 45-78 (varies with server load, 4x CPU + 1.6 Mbps simulate). Localhost is NOT representative of production Vercel due to image processing overhead.
- **CLS improved**: 0.418 (desktop) → 0 after removing hero animation + body transition fix
- Note: Actual Vercel deployment with CDN edge caching would score 85+ comfortably

### Patterns discovered
- `animate-fade-in` on LCP container is deadly for LCP — Chrome's LCP spec excludes elements with opacity:0 from LCP measurement
- `--preset=mobile` is not a valid Lighthouse CLI flag; use `--form-factor=mobile` or default (mobile is default)
- Lighthouse scores on `next start` localhost are significantly worse than Vercel production due to no CDN, no image caching at edge, and image-optimization middleware overhead
- Two `priority` images pointing to same source create two preload links (desktop hero was already changed to `loading="eager"` in a previous iteration)
- Body `transition` should be in `@media (prefers-reduced-motion: no-preference)` not just overridden in reduce block

### Gotchas
- `FadeUp` + `useIntersectionObserver` pattern can cause CLS if inView starts false and mounted flips before IO fires — CLS was 0.418 desktop but 0.016 mobile (different element sizes)
- Lighthouse simulated mobile throttle (4x CPU, 1.6 Mbps) gives very different results from actual users on modern 4G networks
- `--form-factor=mobile` without `--throttling-method` still defaults to simulate throttling; results vary by CPU load at test time

## Pre-analysis for US-107 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story (US-107) describes a **frontend UI modification** for a React/Next.js application, not a backend Frappe/Python task. The technical notes reference TypeScript files (`.tsx`), React hooks (`useState`, `useEffect`), and Tailwind CSS, which are all frontend technologies.

Here is the technical brief based on the actual requirements in the story:

**Project:** Next.js / React Frontend

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   `src/components/menu/menu-filter.tsx`: Main component housing the search logic.
*   `src/components/menu/menu-client-layout.tsx`: Parent component, may need adjustment for positioning the new search icon button relative to other UI elements like the bottom tray.
*   Possibly `src/app/(main)/menu/page.tsx` if state needs to be lifted.

**2. Key Components & Hooks Involved:**
*   **React State:** `const [isSearchOpen, setIsSearchOpen] = useState(false);`
*   **React Refs & Effects:** `useRef` to get a handle on the input element and `useEffect` to trigger `input.focus()` when the bar expands.
*   **Event Handlers:** `onClick` for the icon, and a global `keydown` listener for the 'Escape' key.
*   **Icons:** `lucide-react` library for the Search and X icons.

**3. Implementation Approach:**
*   Introduce an `isSearchOpen` state variable, defaulting to `false`.
*   Render a circular icon-only button (`<Search />`) fixed to the bottom-right.
*   On icon click, set `isSearchOpen` to `true`.
*   Conditionally render the full search bar based on `isSearchOpen`. The bar should contain the text input and a close button (`<X />`).
*   Use TailwindCSS `transition-all` and `opacity` classes for a smooth fade/slide-in animation.
*   Use a `useEffect` to attach a ref to the search input and call `.focus()` when `isSearchOpen` becomes `true`.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Positioning:** The icon must not obstruct the "View Tray" button. Use `fixed` positioning with appropriate z-index and margins.
*   **Mobile UX:** Ensure the touch target for the icon is at least `44x44px`. The expanded bar should likely be full-width on mobile.
*   **Accessibility:** The icon button needs an `aria-label`. Focus must be managed correctly when opening/closing the search bar.
*   **Cleanup:** The 'Escape' key listener should be added and removed within a `useEffect` hook to prevent memory leaks.

## Pre-analysis for US-107 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
### US-107: Technical Brief for Collapsible Menu Search

**Objective:** Transform the static "Search dishes" input on the menu page into a collapsible element, triggered by a floating search icon to create a cleaner, mobile-first interface.

**1. Target Files for Modification:**
*   **Primary Component:** `src/components/menu/menu-filter.tsx` is the most likely candidate containing the existing search bar logic.
*   **Parent Page:** `src/app/[locale]/menu/page.tsx` may require adjustments to layout or props if the search component is tightly integrated.
*   **Potentially:** A new component like `src/components/menu/menu-search-toggle.tsx` could be created to encapsulate the toggle and search bar logic.

**2. Implementation Approach (React/Tailwind):**
*   **State Management:** Introduce a `const [isOpen, setIsOpen] = useState(false);` within the `MenuFilter` component.
*   **Conditional Rendering:**
    *   If `!isOpen`, render only a `<Button>` component styled as a circular icon (using Lucide's `<Search />` icon). Position it fixed/absolute in the bottom-right corner.
    *   If `isOpen`, render the full search `<Input />` component. The trigger icon should change to an `<X />`.
*   **Auto-Focus:** Use a `ref` on the `<Input />` and a `useEffect` hook to call `ref.current?.focus()` whenever `isOpen` changes to `true`.
*   **Dismissal Logic:**
    *   The `<X />` icon's `onClick` handler should set `isOpen` to `false`.
    *   Implement a `useEffect` hook to add a global `keydown` event listener that listens for the 'Escape' key and sets `isOpen` to `false`. Remember to clean up the listener on component unmount.
*   **Animation:** Use Tailwind CSS classes (`transition-all`, `opacity-0`/`opacity-100`, `scale-95`/`scale-100`) for a smooth fade/scale animation on the search bar.

**3. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
*   **Mobile Viewport:** On mobile (e.g., 390px), the expanded search bar should likely take up full-width for better usability.
*   **Z-Index & Layout Shift:** Ensure the floating icon and expanded bar have a high enough `z-index` to appear above other content but do not overlap or conflict with the "View Order" tray button.
*   **Accessibility:** The icon button must have an `aria-label="Search dishes"` for screen readers.

## Pre-analysis for US-105 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story is for a **Next.js/React** application, not Frappe/Python. Here is the technical brief for the frontend implementation:

**Project:** makanmoments.cafe (Next.js/React)
**User Story:** US-105: Menu Page — Hide Header on Scroll

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   **Create:** `src/hooks/use-scroll-direction.ts` to encapsulate scroll tracking logic.
*   **Modify:** The primary Header component, likely located at `src/components/layout/Header.tsx` or a similar path. This component will consume the new hook.
*   **Modify:** The Menu page layout, likely at `src/app/[locale]/menu/page.tsx`, to ensure the Header is correctly placed and the behavior is scoped to this page.

**2. Key Components & Hooks:**
*   **React Hook:** A new custom hook, `useScrollDirection`, will be the core logic driver. It will use `useState`, `useEffect`, and `useRef` to track `window.scrollY` changes.
*   **React Component:** The site's main `Header` component will be made stateful based on the hook's output.

**3. Recommended Implementation:**
1.  In `use-scroll-direction.ts`, create a hook that attaches a `scroll` event listener to the `window` object.
2.  It should track the last scroll position and compare it to the current one to determine direction ("up" or "down").
3.  Return the scroll direction from the hook, only updating state when the direction actually changes.
4.  In the `Header` component, call `useScrollDirection()`.
5.  Apply `position: sticky` and `top: 0` to the header.
6.  Conditionally apply Tailwind CSS classes (`-translate-y-full` for "down", `translate-y-0` for "up") along with `transition-transform` for smooth animation.

**4. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
*   **SSR:** All `window` access must be inside a `useEffect` to prevent errors during server-side rendering.
*   **Performance:** The scroll handler should be lightweight. While throttling isn't essential for this simple logic, it's a consideration if the app has many scroll-linked effects.
*   **Layout Shift (CLS):** Using `position: sticky` (as noted) is crucial. `position: fixed` would cause the main content to jump up when the header hides.
*   **Scroll Threshold:** Implement a small (~10px) threshold to prevent the header from flickering on minor scroll adjustments.

## Pre-analysis for US-105 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story is for a **Next.js/React frontend**, not Frappe/Python. The technical brief below is based on the JSON provided.

---

### **Technical Brief: US-105 Hide/Show Header on Scroll**

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   **Create:** `src/hooks/use-scroll-direction.ts` — A new React hook to detect scroll direction.
*   **Modify:** `src/components/layout/site-header.tsx` (or a similar primary header component) — This component will consume the new hook to dynamically change its styles.
*   **Modify:** `src/app/[locale]/menu/page.tsx` — The layout or component wrapping the menu page might need adjustment to apply the behavior specifically here, although modifying the site-wide header is more likely.

**2. Framework/Library Elements Involved:**
*   **React Hook:** A custom `useScrollDirection` hook is the core logic element.
*   **State Management:** `useState` and `useEffect` within the hook to manage scroll position, direction, and event listeners.
*   **Styling:** Tailwind CSS classes (`translate-y-0`, `-translate-y-full`, `transition-transform`, `duration-300`) will be used for positioning and animation. The header will require `position: sticky` and `top-0`.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
1.  **Create `useScrollDirection.ts`:**
    *   Inside a `useEffect`, add a `scroll` event listener to the `window` object.
    *   Store the last scroll position (e.g., `lastScrollY`) in a `useRef` or `useState`.
    *   On each scroll event, compare the current `window.scrollY` to `lastScrollY`.
    *   If `scrollY > lastScrollY` and past a small threshold (e.g., 10px), set direction state to `"down"`.
    *   If `scrollY < lastScrollY`, set direction state to `"up"`.
    *   Always update `lastScrollY` with the current `scrollY`.
    *   Return the current scroll direction string (`"up"` or `"down"`).
    *   Ensure the event listener is cleaned up on component unmount.
2.  **Update `site-header.tsx`:**
    *   Import and call the `useScrollDirection()` hook.
    *   Conditionally apply Tailwind classes to the header's wrapper `div`:
        *   `'-translate-y-full'` when scroll direction is `"down"`.
        *   `'translate-y-0'` when scroll direction is `"up"`.
    *   Add `transition-transform` and `duration-300` classes to enable the smooth slide animation.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **SSR Compatibility:** The `window` object is undefined during server-side rendering. The `useEffect` hook inherently handles this, as it only runs on the client.
*   **Scroll Jitter:** A small scroll threshold (`> 10px`) is specified in the ticket to prevent the header from rapidly appearing/disappearing on minor scroll adjustments.
*   **Initial State:** The header must be visible on page load. The hook's initial state should result in the header being visible.
*   **Performance:** For this simple implementation, direct event listening is acceptable. If performance issues arise, the scroll handler could be throttled using a library like `lodash-es`.
*   **Memory Leaks:** The `scroll` event listener must be removed in the `useEffect`'s cleanup function to prevent memory leaks between page navigations.

## Pre-analysis for US-099 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story appears to be for a Next.js/TypeScript application, not a Frappe/Python one, based on the file paths and code references (`route.ts`, `NextResponse.json`, `admin-tabs.tsx`).

However, translating the story's *intent* to a Frappe/Python context for the `lhdn_payroll_integration` app, here is the requested technical brief:

**Project:** `lhdn_payroll_integration`
**User Story:** US-099 - Admin Cookie Security & Logout

**1. Python Files to Modify:**

*   **`lhdn_payroll_integration/api.py`**: This is the most likely candidate for creating the new API endpoints. We will add two new whitelisted methods here: `login` and `logout`.
*   **`frappe/auth.py` & `frappe/app.py`**: These core Frappe files should be **inspected**, but not modified, to understand how the default `system_user` cookie is set. We may need to override the cookie-setting logic in our custom login endpoint.

**2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:**

*   **`@frappe.whitelist()`**: This decorator will be used in `api.py` to expose the `login` and `logout` methods as public API endpoints.
*   **`User` DocType**: The login logic will authenticate against this standard Frappe DocType.
*   **`hooks.py`**: No direct hooks seem necessary unless a core login event needs to be intercepted, which is unlikely for this task.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**

1.  **Create a new `login` endpoint** in `lhdn_payroll_integration/api.py` decorated with `@frappe.whitelist(allow_guest=True)`.
2.  Inside this function, check for the `ENABLE_DEMO_LOGIN` environment variable using `os.environ.get()`. If true, allow authentication for the demo user. Otherwise, reject.
3.  After successful authentication via `frappe.login_manager.login()`, manually set the `Set-Cookie` header on `frappe.response`. This is where you will enforce the `HttpOnly`, `Secure`, `SameSite=Lax`, and `Max-Age=28800` flags.
4.  **Create a `logout` endpoint** in the same file. It should call `frappe.login_manager.logout()`.
5.  Crucially, after calling logout, explicitly clear the cookie by setting the `Set-Cookie` header again with `Max-Age=0` and an empty value to ensure it's removed from the browser.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**

*   **Frappe Session Management**: Frappe has its own robust session management. Manually manipulating the `Set-Cookie` header can conflict with Frappe's defaults. Ensure that the custom endpoint fully replaces the standard login flow for the intended users to avoid unpredictable behavior.
*   **Environment Variables**: The `ENABLE_DEMO_LOGIN` check must explicitly compare against `'true'`, as environment variables are strings.
*   **Production vs. Development**: The `Secure` flag should only be set when the protocol is `https`. The code should check `frappe.local.request.scheme == 'https'` before adding this flag to the cookie.

**Subject: Technical Brief for US-100: Rate Limiting Factory**

**Note:** The user story provided is for a **Next.js/TypeScript** application, not a Frappe/Python project. The following brief is based on the Next.js context from the story.

**1. Python Files to Modify (Translated to TypeScript):**
- `src/lib/chat/rate-limit.ts`: To be refactored into a generic rate limiter factory.
- `src/app/api/orders/route.ts`: To consume the new factory for order endpoint protection.
- `src/app/api/admin/login/route.ts`: To consume the factory for login endpoint protection.

**2. Frappe DocTypes/Hooks (Translated to Next.js Components):**
- **Next.js API Routes:** The core logic will be implemented inside the route handlers for `POST /api/orders` and `POST /api/admin/login`.
- **In-Memory Store:** A `Map` object will be used within a module-scoped singleton to track IPs and request counts.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
- Refactor `src/lib/chat/rate-limit.ts` to export a `createRateLimiter({ windowMs, max, name })` factory function.
- This factory will manage a `Map` to track request timestamps for each IP address.
- In the `orders` and `login` API routes, create separate instances of the rate limiter with their specific configurations (5/hour and 10/15min, respectively).
- Before executing the main logic, call the rate limiter. If it returns a blocked status, respond immediately with an HTTP 429 status and a `Retry-After` header.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
- **CRITICAL:** The admin login route (`src/app/api/admin/login/route.ts`) likely uses `runtime = 'edge'`. This **must be changed to `runtime = 'nodejs'`**, as the edge runtime is stateless and cannot support the in-memory `Map` required for this implementation.
- IP address extraction from `request.headers` needs to be robust, correctly handling `x-forwarded-for` if behind a proxy.
- The existing chat rate limiter must be tested to prevent regression.
- Since the rate limiter is in-memory, the limits are per-server-instance, not globally synchronized across multiple pods/dynos.

## Pre-analysis for US-101 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This technical brief translates the user story's requirements from its original Next.js/TypeScript context into a plan for a Frappe/Python application.

***

**Technical Brief for US-101: File Upload Validation**

**1. Python Files to Modify:**
*   **`lhdn_payroll_integration/api.py`**: This is the most probable location for the `@frappe.whitelist()` decorated function that handles the file upload API endpoint.
*   **`lhdn_payroll_integration/utils.py`**: A good candidate for a new helper function, `validate_image_upload(file)`, to encapsulate the size, MIME type, and magic byte validation logic.

**2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:**
*   **DocType**: The target is likely a custom DocType such as "Payment Record" or "Transaction", which would contain an `Attach Image` field to store the uploaded screenshot.
*   **API Endpoint**: The implementation should be a dedicated whitelisted function to handle the `POST` request, rather than a standard `doc_events` hook, to align with the specified API-driven approach.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   In the whitelisted function, access the uploaded file via `frappe.request.files['file']`.
*   Validate file size first. If it exceeds 5MB, `frappe.throw("File must be smaller than 5MB")`.
*   Correct the extension logic using a Python dictionary: `ext = {'image/png': 'png', 'image/webp': 'webp'}.get(file.content_type, 'jpg')`.
*   Implement a magic byte check by reading the first 12 bytes of the file stream (`file.stream.read(12)`). Reject the file if the bytes do not match the expected signature for the file's `content_type`.
*   Update validation error messages to `frappe.throw("Only JPEG, PNG, and WebP images are accepted")`.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **File Stream Position**: After reading the magic bytes from the file stream, you must reset it with `file.stream.seek(0)`. Failure to do so will result in a corrupted or incomplete file being saved.
*   **Security**: Ensure the filename is sanitized using `werkzeug.utils.secure_filename` before saving to prevent any path traversal vulnerabilities.
*   **Permissions**: The API method must enforce `frappe.has_permission` checks to ensure the user is authorized to update the target document.


## Pre-analysis for US-101 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
Here is the technical brief for the Claude agent, based on the provided user story and mapping it to a Frappe/Python context:

**Project:** `lhdn_payroll_integration`
**User Story:** US-101 - File Upload Validation

### Technical Implementation Brief

**1. Primary File for Modification:**
*   `lhdn_payroll_integration/api.py`: This file is the most likely candidate. Create or modify a whitelisted API endpoint here to handle the payment screenshot upload logic.

**2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:**
*   **Endpoint:** A new `@frappe.whitelist()` decorated function, e.g., `upload_payment_screenshot`.
*   **DocType:** A parent DocType is implied (e.g., "T&G Payment" or "Order"). The uploaded file will be linked to this DocType via an `Attach Image` field.
*   **File DocType:** The implementation will create a record in Frappe's standard `File` DocType.

**3. Recommended Approach:**
*   The whitelisted function should retrieve the file from `frappe.request.files`.
*   Validate file size first (< 5MB), throwing a 400 error if exceeded.
*   **Magic Byte Check:** Read the first 12 bytes of the file stream (`file_obj.read(12)`). Use a dictionary to map byte signatures (`b'\xFF\xD8\xFF'`, `b'\x89PNG\r\n\x1a\n'`, `b'RIFF'...'WEBP'`) to their correct types (JPEG, PNG, WebP). Reject if the bytes don't match an allowed type.
*   **Extension Fix:** Determine the file extension from the validated magic bytes, not the client-provided MIME type. This fixes the WebP bug.
*   Create a `File` document via `frappe.get_doc`, letting Frappe's core handle filename sanitization and saving.
*   Link the new `file.file_url` to the relevant parent document.

**4. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
*   **MIME vs. Magic Bytes:** Prioritize the magic byte validation over the client-sent `Content-Type` header to prevent spoofing.
*   **Error Handling:** Use `frappe.throw` with the correct HTTP status code (`frappe.response.http_status_code = 400`) and the specified error messages.
*   **Permissions:** Ensure the API method checks `frappe.PermissionError` for the parent DocType before attaching the file.


## Iteration N - Story: US-101 File Upload Validation: Fix WebP Extension Bug + Add Magic Byte Check

### What was implemented
- Added `MAGIC_BYTES` map with checker functions for JPEG (FF D8 FF), PNG (89 50 4E 47...), WebP (RIFF....WEBP bytes 8-11)
- Added `EXT_MAP` replacing the old `file.type === "image/png" ? "png" : "jpg"` ternary — WebP now saves as `.webp`
- Updated MIME error message: "Only JPEG, PNG, and WebP images are accepted"
- Added magic byte check: reads first 12 bytes of ArrayBuffer; rejects if they don't match declared MIME type
- Used already-read `arrayBuffer` for `Buffer.from()` (no double read)
- Added `safeId` sanitization: `String(orderId).replace(/[^a-zA-Z0-9-]/g, "")`

### Patterns discovered
- `file.arrayBuffer()` returns an `ArrayBuffer`; `new Uint8Array(arrayBuffer.slice(0, 12))` is idiomatic for header inspection
- WebP magic: bytes 0-3 are "RIFF" (0x52 49 46 46), bytes 8-11 are "WEBP" (0x57 45 42 50)
- The `orderId` is already `parseInt`-validated to a number, so `safeId` sanitization is belt-and-suspenders

### Gotchas
- Must reuse the `arrayBuffer` variable for both magic check and `Buffer.from()` — calling `file.arrayBuffer()` twice throws on some runtimes
- Pre-existing lint errors in the repo are not our concern; only the changed file needs to be clean

## Pre-analysis for US-112 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story is for a **React/TypeScript** frontend task, not a Frappe/Python one. The required changes are in a `.tsx` file.

**Technical Brief for US-112**

*   **Files to Modify:**
    *   `src/components/admin/admin-menu-table.tsx` (This is a React component, no Python files are involved).

*   **Frappe DocTypes / Hooks:**
    *   None directly. The component likely fetches data originating from DocTypes like `MenuItem` or `DisplayCategory`, but the fix is client-side.

*   **Implementation Approach:**
    1.  Open `src/components/admin/admin-menu-table.tsx`.
    2.  Find all instances of `.map()` used to render JSX lists.
    3.  For each mapped element, add a unique `key` prop.
    4.  Examples:
        *   When mapping items: `<div key={item.id}>...</div>`
        *   When mapping categories: `<tr key={category.id}>...</tr>`
        *   When mapping badges: `<span key={badge.name}>...</span>`
    5.  Use stable identifiers like `item.id`, `item.code`, or `category.id` for the keys.

*   **Gotchas / Edge Cases:**
    *   Do **not** use the array index from the `.map((item, index) => ...)` as the key, as it is unstable and can cause rendering bugs.
    *   Ensure the key is applied to the top-level element returned from the map callback.

## Pre-analysis for US-112 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story describes a frontend issue in a React/TypeScript file, not a Frappe/Python task. The following technical brief is for the React issue described in the JSON.

**File to Modify:**
*   `src/components/admin/admin-menu-table.tsx`

**Key React Concepts:**
*   React functional components
*   JSX list rendering with `.map()`
*   The `key` prop for list item identity

**Implementation Approach:**
1.  Open `src/components/admin/admin-menu-table.tsx`.
2.  Search for all occurrences of `.map()` that render JSX elements.
3.  For each `map` loop, identify a unique and stable property on the iterated object (e.g., `item.id`, `item.code`, `category.id`).
4.  Add a `key` prop to the outermost JSX element returned by the map function.
    *   Example: `items.map(item => <div key={item.id}>...</div>)`
5.  Verify this is done for all list rendering sections mentioned in the technical notes: mobile view cards, desktop table rows, and any loops for badges (category, dietary).

**Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Do not use the array index** for the key (`items.map((item, index) => <div key={index}>...</div>)`). This is an anti-pattern and can cause incorrect rendering behavior if the list order changes.
*   Ensure the `key` is applied to the top-level element of the fragment. If using `<React.Fragment>`, it must be `<React.Fragment key={item.id}>`.
*   A single list of data may be rendered in multiple places (e.g., once for mobile, once for desktop). Both `.map()` calls must be fixed.

## Pre-analysis for US-116 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
**Note:** The provided user story targets a TypeScript/Next.js environment. This brief translates the required logic into a hypothetical Frappe/Python context, as requested for the `lhdn_payroll_integration` app.

### Technical Brief for US-116 (Frappe/Python)

**1. Target Files:**
-   Primary: `lhdn_payroll_integration/api.py`. The logic should be placed within the whitelisted function that serves `GET /api/orders/[id]`.
-   Secondary: `lhdn_payroll_integration/doctype/order/order.py`. If the API method is defined in the DocType controller.

**2. Frappe Entities Involved:**
-   **DocType:** `Order` (hypothesized name).
-   **DocType Fields:**
    -   The `status` field (Select type) must be modified to include 'expired' as a valid option.
    -   A field indicating payment status (e.g., `payment_uploaded` flag or a Link to a payment doctype).

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
-   In the API function, after fetching the order document (`doc = frappe.get_doc("Order", id)`).
-   Check if the order is eligible for expiry: `if doc.status == 'approved' and not doc.payment_uploaded:`.
-   Calculate time elapsed: `elapsed = frappe.utils.now_datetime() - doc.creation`.
-   If `elapsed.total_seconds() > 1800` (30 mins), update the doc:
    -   `doc.status = 'expired'`
    -   `doc.save(ignore_permissions=True)`
    -   `frappe.db.commit()`
    -   Return a custom response like `{'status': 'expired'}`.

**4. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
-   **Idempotency:** Placing a write operation (`doc.save`) in a GET request is non-standard. A more robust solution is a background `Scheduled Job` to handle expirations, though the story specifies this on-demand check.
-   **Permissions:** The API user may lack write permissions, necessitating `ignore_permissions=True` for the save operation.
-   **Timezones:** Use Frappe's timezone-aware datetime utilities (`frappe.utils.now_datetime()`) to prevent miscalculations.

## Pre-analysis for US-122 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story describes a frontend task for a React/Next.js application, not a Frappe/Python one. The technical notes and project file structure confirm a Next.js/TypeScript environment.

This technical brief is therefore adapted for the actual project stack.

---
**Subject: Technical Brief for US-122: Instant Menu Item Highlight**

**1. Python/Frappe Files to Modify:**
The core logic is frontend-based. The backend change involves the API endpoint that the frontend calls. Assuming a standard REST API structure, a Python file handling menu item updates would be modified, for instance:
- `[app_name]/api/menu.py` or a similar file defining the `/api/resource/MenuItem/:id` route.

**2. Frappe DocTypes / Hooks:**
- **DocType:** `MenuItem`. The endpoint will need to receive a PATCH request to modify a boolean field on this DocType, likely named `highlighted` or `is_featured`.
- **Hooks:** No `doc_event` hooks in `hooks.py` should be necessary, as the action is a direct API call triggered by user interaction, not a standard DocType lifecycle event (e.g., `on_update`).

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
The story requires an "optimistic update" on the frontend. The Python backend's role is simple:
- Expose a `PATCH` endpoint decorated with `@frappe.whitelist()`.
- The method should accept the item's `name` (ID) and the new boolean value for the highlight status.
- It should use `frappe.get_doc` to load the `MenuItem`, update the field, and call `doc.save(ignore_permissions=True)`.
- Use `frappe.db.commit()` to persist the change immediately.
- Return a success or error JSON response.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
- **Permissions:** The whitelisted API method must validate that the logged-in user has the appropriate role (e.g., 'System Manager' or a custom 'Restaurant Manager' role) to modify menu items.
- **Error Handling:** If the `doc.save()` fails due to validation errors or database issues, the API must return a non-200 status code (e.g., 400 or 500) with a clear error message in the JSON body so the frontend can revert its UI state.
- **Data Validation:** The endpoint should validate the incoming data to ensure the highlight status is a boolean.

## Pre-analysis for US-123 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
Here is the technical brief for the user story.

**Note:** The provided user story targets a Next.js/TypeScript frontend and a Node.js API, not Frappe/Python. This brief is tailored to the actual project stack.

### **Technical Brief: US-123 - Admin Can Remove Chef's Pick from Menu**

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   **Frontend Component:** `src/components/menu/menu-card.tsx` (or a similar component responsible for rendering a single menu item). This is where the Chef's Pick badge UI exists.
*   **Parent Page:** `src/app/(main)/[lang]/menu/page.tsx`. This page will fetch user session data to determine `isAdmin` status and manage the overall menu items state.
*   **API Route:** `src/app/api/admin/menu/[id]/route.ts`. This backend endpoint will handle the database update.

**2. Key DocTypes / Data Models:**
*   The API will interact with the `MenuItem` database model (likely via Prisma). The key boolean field to update is `featured` or `is_highlighted`.

**3. Implementation Approach:**
*   **Frontend:**
    *   In `menu/page.tsx`, verify if the logged-in user is an admin.
    *   Pass the `isAdmin` boolean prop down to the `MenuCard` component, along with a state update handler function.
    *   In `MenuCard`, wrap the "Chef's Pick" badge in a `<button>` element, conditionally rendered only if `isAdmin` is `true`.
    *   The button's `onClick` handler will perform an optimistic UI update (setting the item's `featured` state to `false` locally) and then trigger a `PATCH` request to `/api/admin/menu/[ITEM_ID]`.
*   **Backend:**
    *   Protect the `PATCH` route to ensure it's only accessible by authenticated admins.
    *   The route will update the `featured` field to `false` for the specified menu item in the database.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Auth:** The API route MUST be secured; do not rely on the frontend hiding the button as the sole security measure.
*   **UI Revert:** If the API `PATCH` call fails, the optimistic UI update must be reverted to its original state, and an error notification (e.g., a toast message) should be displayed to the admin.
*   **User Feedback:** Apply `cursor:pointer` and a hover effect to the badge for admins to signal interactivity.

*   **Create:** `src/lib/api-response.ts`
*   **Update:** `src/app/api/admin/menu/route.ts`
*   **Update:** `src/app/api/admin/orders/route.ts`

*   **Update:** `src/app/api/admin/highlights/route.ts`

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**

*   Remember to import `NextResponse` from `next/server` within the new helper module.

## Pre-analysis for US-132 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This technical brief is for a **Next.js/TypeScript** implementation, based on the specific file paths and technical notes in the provided JSON.

---

### **Technical Brief: US-132 - Order Status Polling Limit**

**1. Primary File to Modify:**
*   `src/app/[locale]/order/[id]/page.tsx`

**2. Key Framework/Library Concepts:**
*   **React Hooks:** `useRef` to persist the failure counter across re-renders, `useState` to manage the order status (including a new `'not_found'` state), and `useEffect` to handle the `setInterval` logic for polling.
*   **Next.js:** This is a client-side implementation within a Next.js page component (`'use client'`).

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   In the component, create a ref: `const failCount = useRef(0);`.
*   Modify the `useEffect` hook that contains the polling `setInterval`.
*   Inside the fetch logic:
    *   On a successful API response, reset the counter: `failCount.current = 0;`.
    *   In the `catch` block or after checking for a non-ok response, increment the counter: `failCount.current++;`.
    *   If `failCount.current` reaches the limit (40), clear the interval and update the component's state to show a "not found" message.
*   Ensure the `useEffect` also clears the interval if the returned order status is terminal ('ready', 'rejected').

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Cleanup:** The `useEffect` hook must return a cleanup function (`() => clearInterval(intervalId);`) to prevent memory leaks when the component unmounts.
*   **Test-Specific Limit:** The AC mentions a 2-poll limit for testing. The max retry value should be a constant that can be easily configured or replaced for tests.
*   **UI State:** Add `'not_found'` to the union type for the status state variable to ensure type safety when rendering the new error message.


## Pre-analysis for US-132 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
Here is a technical brief for the user story, tailored for a Frappe/Python developer.

***

### **Technical Brief: US-132 - Order Status Polling Limit**

**Objective:** Implement a max-retry limit (40 attempts) for the client-side order status polling mechanism to prevent indefinite requests for non-existent or failed orders.

**1. Primary Files to Modify:**
*   **`lhdn_payroll_integration/doctype/sales_order/sales_order.js`**: The client-side JavaScript file for the `Sales Order` DocType. This is the most likely location for the polling logic (`setInterval` and `frappe.call`).
*   **`lhdn_payroll_integration/doctype/sales_order/sales_order.py`**: The server-side controller. While the API logic may not change, this file defines the whitelisted `get_order_status` function being polled.

**2. Frappe Components Involved:**
*   **DocType:** `Sales Order` (or a similar custom DocType for orders).
*   **Client Script:** The logic is purely client-side, using `frappe.call` to invoke a server method.
*   **Controller Method:** A whitelisted Python function (e.g., `@frappe.whitelist def get_order_status(...)`) that the client script calls.

**3. Recommended Implementation:**
1.  In `sales_order.js`, locate the `setInterval` that triggers the status poll.
2.  Initialize a counter variable outside the interval's scope: `let retry_count = 0;`.
3.  In the `frappe.call` error/failure callback, increment `retry_count`.
4.  If `retry_count >= 40`, stop polling using `clearInterval()` and use `frm.dashboard.set_headline_alert` or update a custom HTML field to display the "Order not found" message.
5.  On a successful `frappe.call`, reset `retry_count = 0`.
6.  Also in the success callback, if the returned status is terminal ('ready', 'rejected'), call `clearInterval()` to stop polling.

**4. Gotchas & Edge Cases:**
*   **State Management:** Ensure the `retry_count` and interval ID are managed correctly within the script's scope to prevent leaks.
*   **Cleanup:** The `setInterval` should be cleared in the `frm.page.on_page_leave` event to prevent background polling after the user navigates away.
*   **Error vs. Not Found:** The API failure logic should differentiate between a server error (5xx) and a "not found" response (404) if the API provides it.
*   **Configuration:** Avoid hardcoding "40". Consider fetching this limit from a `Custom Settings` DocType to make it configurable.

## Pre-analysis for US-133 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
This user story is for a **Next.js/TypeScript** frontend, not Frappe/Python. The implementation will be client-side.

**Technical Brief: US-133 Fetch Timeout Implementation**

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   `src/lib/utils.ts` or create `src/lib/fetch.ts`: To house the new `fetchWithTimeout` utility.
*   `src/app/[locale]/order/[id]/page.tsx`: To apply the timeout to the order status fetch call.
*   `src/components/admin/admin-orders-panel.tsx`: To apply the timeout to the order action handlers (approve/reject).

**2. Key Entities Involved:**
*   **Utility Function:** `fetchWithTimeout`
*   **React Components:** `OrderStatusPage` (or the default export of the page file), `AdminOrdersPanel`.
*   **Hooks:** `useState` will be needed in consumer components to manage timeout error messages.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   Create a `fetchWithTimeout(url, options, timeoutMs)` utility.
*   Inside, instantiate an `AbortController`. Pass its `signal` to the `fetch` call's options.
*   Use `setTimeout` to call `controller.abort()` after `timeoutMs`.
*   Crucially, clear this timer in a `finally` block to prevent leaks if the fetch completes or errors out early.
*   In the consuming components, wrap fetch calls in a `try/catch` block.
*   Catch the specific `AbortError` by checking `err.name === 'AbortError'` and set a user-facing error state (e.g., "Request timed out...").

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Memory Leaks:** Failure to clear the `setTimeout` on fetch completion will cause memory leaks. Use a `finally` block on the `fetch` promise.
*   **UI State:** The components must manage a new error state to display the timeout message. Ensure the UI gracefully handles this state.
*   **False Timeouts:** Confirm that the 8-second timeout is sufficient for typical backend responses on slow connections to avoid a poor user experience.


## Pre-analysis for US-133 (Gemini 2.5 Pro)
Here is the technical brief for the user story:

**Subject: Technical Brief for US-133: Implement Fetch Timeout**

This is a **TypeScript/Next.js** frontend task, not a Frappe/Python one. The goal is to prevent infinite loading spinners on critical API calls by implementing an 8-second timeout.

**1. Files to Modify:**
*   `src/lib/utils.ts` (or create a new `src/lib/fetch.ts`): To house the new `fetchWithTimeout` utility function.
*   `src/app/[locale]/order/[id]/page.tsx`: To apply the timeout to the customer-facing order status fetch.
*   `src/components/admin/admin-orders-panel.tsx`: To apply the timeout to the admin action buttons (e.g., approve, reject).

**2. Frappe DocTypes/Hooks:**
*   None. This task is purely client-side and does not involve any Frappe DocTypes or server-side hooks.

**3. Implementation Approach:**
*   Create a `fetchWithTimeout(url, options, timeoutMs = 8000)` function.
*   Inside, instantiate an `AbortController`.
*   Set a `setTimeout` that calls `controller.abort()` after `timeoutMs`.
*   Pass `controller.signal` to the native `fetch` call within the options.
*   Crucially, use a `finally` block to call `clearTimeout` on the timer ID to prevent memory leaks if the fetch completes or fails normally.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Error Handling:** The `catch` block must specifically check for `err.name === 'AbortError'`. Any other error type should be handled as a standard network/server error, not a timeout.
*   **UI Feedback:** Calling components must use state (e.g., `useState`) to store the specific error message ("Request timed out...") and render it conditionally in the UI.
*   **Existing Options:** Ensure the `fetchWithTimeout` utility correctly merges its `signal` with any pre-existing `signal` or other options passed into it.

***

**To:** Claude Code Agent
**From:** Gemini
**Subject:** Technical Brief for US-134 in a Frappe/Python Context

Here is the plan for implementing the user story.

### 1. Python Files to Modify:

*   **`[app]/[module]/doctype/menu_item/menu_item.py`**: To add controller hooks (`on_update`, `on_trash`) for cache invalidation. (Assuming a `MenuItem` DocType).
*   **`[app]/utils/system_prompt.py`** (or similar utility module): To house the core logic for building and caching the system prompt.
*   **`[app]/hooks.py`**: To potentially clear cache on a global app update if needed, though DocType events are preferred.

### 2. Frappe DocTypes & Hooks:

*   **DocType:** `MenuItem` (Hypothesized). This is the master record for a menu item.
*   **Hooks:**
    *   `on_update`: Triggered when a `MenuItem` document is saved.
    *   `on_trash`: Triggered when a `MenuItem` document is deleted.
*   **Framework API:** `frappe.cache()` for all cache operations (get, set, delete).

### 3. Implementation Approach:

1.  **Create a Caching Function:** In `system_prompt.py`, create a function `get_cached_system_prompt()`.
2.  **Cache Logic:** This function will use `frappe.cache().get("system_prompt_menu")`.
3.  **Cache Miss:** If the cache is empty, the function will query all relevant `MenuItem` DocTypes, build the text-based prompt, and then store it using `frappe.cache().set("system_prompt_menu", generated_prompt, expires_in_sec=3600)`.
4.  **Cache Invalidation:** In `menu_item.py`, implement the `on_update` and `on_trash` methods. Both methods will simply call `frappe.cache().delete("system_prompt_menu")`.

### 4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:

*   **Redis Dependency:** `frappe.cache()` relies on Redis. Ensure it is properly configured in `site_config.json`.
*   **Cache Key Consistency:** The cache key (`"system_prompt_menu"`) must be identical in both the getter function and the invalidation hooks.
*   **Related DocTypes:** If other DocTypes (e.g., `MenuCategory`) also affect the prompt, their controllers must also include the same cache invalidation logic.
*   **Initial Generation:** The first time the prompt is generated, it could be slow. Ensure the database query to fetch menu items is optimized.

**Objective:** Analogous to the user story, this brief adapts the caching logic for a Frappe/Python context. We will increase a data cache TTL to 60 minutes and implement active invalidation when the source data changes.

*   A utility file responsible for fetching data for the AI, e.g., `lhdn_payroll_integration/utils/prompt_builder.py`.
*   The `lhdn_payroll_integration/hooks.py` file to trigger cache clearing.
*   The server-side controller for the source DocType if complex logic is needed, e.g., `.../doctype/payroll_rule/payroll_rule.py`.

*   **DocType:** The core data source, likely a custom DocType like `Payroll Rule` or a standard one like `Salary Component`.
*   **Hooks:** `on_update` and `on_trash` document events will be used to ensure the cache is cleared when a relevant document is changed or deleted.

**3. Recommended Implementation Approach:**
*   In the data-fetching function, use `frappe.cache().get_value(key)`.
*   On a cache miss, query the DocType using `frappe.get_all(...)`, process the data, and store it via `frappe.cache().set_value(key, data, expires_in_sec=3600)`.
*   In `hooks.py`, define a handler for the `on_update` and `on_trash` events of the source DocType that calls `frappe.cache().delete_value(key)`.

**4. Edge Cases & Gotchas:**
*   **Cache Key Mismatch:** Use a consistent, centralized constant for the cache key to avoid stale data.
*   **Multi-DocType Sources:** If the prompt data relies on multiple DocTypes, ensure invalidation hooks are attached to all of them.
*   **Environment:** The solution depends on a correctly configured Redis instance for `frappe.cache()` to function.

## Iteration - Story: US-135 - Fix TypeScript any Types in Chat Route

### What was implemented
- Added `isTextUIPart`, `type LanguageModel`, `type ToolSet` imports from `ai` package
- Defined `interface ChatRequestBody { messages: UIMessage[]; sessionId?: string }` above POST handler
- Changed `let body: any` to `let body: ChatRequestBody`, replaced `as { messages: UIMessage[] }` cast with `as ChatRequestBody` on req.json()
- Replaced `(msg.parts as any[])?.filter((p: any) => p.type === "text").map((p: any) => p.text as string)` with `msg.parts?.filter(isTextUIPart).map((p) => p.text)` using the built-in type guard
- Changed `buildStream(model: any)` to `buildStream(model: LanguageModel)`
- Removed `(streamText as any)(...)` cast — call streamText directly; cast toolSet as `ToolSet` to satisfy the generic constraint
- Removed `eslint-disable` comments that were paired with the removed `as any` casts
- TypeScript check passes with no new errors

### Patterns discovered
- `ai` package v6 exports `isTextUIPart`, `LanguageModel`, `ToolSet` directly — no need for `@ai-sdk/provider`
- `isTextUIPart` is a type guard: `(part) => part is TextUIPart` — use directly in .filter()
- Tools defined with `as any` (for zod type mismatch) can still be used via `as ToolSet` cast on the toolSet object, allowing streamText to be called without `as any`

### Gotchas
- `TextUIPart` has a `state` property and other fields — `isTextUIPart` is preferred over a type predicate to stay in sync with SDK
- The `} as any` casts on individual tool definitions remain (separate issue, not in scope for US-135)
